RATING: 10
Go rivals chess for intensity and complexity. Go's rules are actually simpler than chess, but its very human-ness makes it a very difficult game to get a computer to play. Weak chess players find it miserable to play against someone better, but Go's handicap system makes it possible for weak and strong players to sit down to a game that challenges both. If you're looking for the challenges and camaraderie of something like a chess club but without the extreme stratification you find with chess, Go might be your game.

RATING: 7
Simple rules, complex strategy. This game rewards long-term strategic planning as well as tactical finesse. An excellent handicapping system allows players of disparate skill levels to play each other fairly evenly, though the depth of play can be intimidating for beginners. 

However, the game may be too deep to warrant regular play from me. I feel it's similar to Chess in the way that study and memorization of specific game states greatly improves one's skill at the game, Go's handicapping and lack of arbitrary rules makes the game significantly more appealing for me. I just can't give the game the attention it deserves and balance it against other interests.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One of the most aesthetically pleasing games. A carefully handcrafted board with real polished stones can be a true gem. I would like to own one just for the looks.

When it comes to the game itself, the rules are deceivingly simple. Just play a stone on any vacant intersection on the board. Of course there are some illegal moves (the ko rule, or suicide). However, once you start playing that assessing the situation becomes very very hard. After several of plays it might still be a bit unclear whether you do have an area in your control or not.

I can clearly see why Go can capture the entire life of individuals. The option space is stupefyingly vast, and there is so much to learn. I reckon learning a set of cornerplays will help a lot, because then you will have automatic answers to certain openings/invasions thus saving your energy to the battles that have no clear answers.

RATING: 6
It's hard to give this a rating.  I don't enjoy it as such, but it is clearly a good game.  I don't think that it has any obvious flaws apart from the fact that it's VERY abstract and if you don't like really dry abstracts then it's one to avoid.  And this certainly is drier than most abstracts, for my taste.  

Personally, I'd much rather play Yinsh, Hive or Billabong, but I can't say that this is worth less than 6.  I simply don't enjoy it enough to rate it higher than that.

Certainly a brain-burner, and having played it I can see where some of the ideas in games like Through the Desert and other Knizia's in particular come from.

RATING: 7
The abstract strategy game with the most depth.  Rather simple rules and very profound strategy.  But it is really a very heavy game which is not very accessible to novice players: you really need to be taught by a good coach (or read a good book) in order to "get started" into the game, kind of like golf or tennis; if you just learn the rules and try to explore the game on your own, it's probably a waste of your time.  A bit over-analysed by the experts, which sometimes undermines the enjoyment a bit for casual players (like me).  The best game for serious intellectual challenge, clearly above chess and the other classics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I was first exposed to Go in college in the context of a Machine Learning class, I found the game quite interesting and played a few games w/ professors and later a local club.  After having not played Go in several years, I found my board at my parents house and decided to teach my friends to play.  After spending an entire weekend teaching/playing Go, and then playing a friend who is at a peer level, I've decided to re-evaluate Go.

Rating this game is easy; it pretty much defines 10.  The level of intensity and focus that I experience in the game is unmatched by any other game.  The various board sizes (9x9, 13x13, 19x19) allow control over game duration.  The handicapping system is brilliant since it allows both the more and less experienced players to have a challenging experience. I've found explaining the rules is fairly easy, although it takes some time to understand 'alive' vs. 'dead' shapes and strategy.  Still, my friends are progressing rapidly and having a lot of learning.  I'm having a blast playing Go again, too.  Heck, I even had fun playing against myself one night!

I think a lot of people are intimidated by Go (and Chess to a degree) due to the competitive organized play.  I don't really care about such, and highly recommend Go for any gamer from casual to whatever.  Additionally, I feel that a moderate understanding of Go is very useful for a wide variety of other games with aspects of spatial area control (ex. Settler of Catan and relatives, Through the Desert, etc).  Overall, I simply feel that Go is an amazing game with incredible depth, yet a lot of inherent flexibility.  Go is, to me, the fundamental game and I highly recommend that every gamer be (at least) familiar with this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Ultra competitive tournament game with no ceiling for skill.

Complexity - none, extremely simple rules

Strategy - unlimited, better player will win

Luck - none

Interaction - extreme, every move you make effects the other player

Fun - varies, there is a handicap system to bring skill levels closer together but varied skills still have a hard time both having fun

Do not like it. Or rather i do not like the sort of players that it attracts. It mostly attracts elitist folk incapable of playing or enjoying other boardgames. And seriously this debate that Go is better than Chess based on the fact that humans can still prevail over machines, is stupid. It's like comparing apples to oranges and declaring one or the other superior based on some arbitrary characteristic.

These two games are not similar at all. Chess emphasizes on the relative power of different pieces in a fluctuating board dominated by the control of the centre and better mobility. Go on the other hand does not require you to control the centre to win. On the contrary it is a serious disadvantage to play in the centre too much because you can be undermined by the flanks. There is no concept of mobility in Go. Also the fluctuation of the position is limited, i.e. there is no maneuvering. 
Last but not least, i cannot possibly stop myself from laughing when i see that the game is 4000 yr old. Yes the basic principles are but the current scoring ruleset is what? 100 year old tops? Without the current scoring methods older games played in earlier times could end differently. 
Also there is no concept of a draw in Go. This is not good and reduces the strategic scope of the game but it is of no use describing why in this forum.

To be fair the game is not bad but definately not the "best game evah". And to be frank neither chess is. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Sacrilege, I know, but I do not like this one. For all the talk of elegant rules, Go still has to patch up gaps like ko, which requires previous game state to be recalled.

But my biggest problem with the game is my teachers - I've never played against someone who has been willing to give me advice, or divulge overall strategic considerations. Consequentially, I can't read board state to see what battle is already lost, I have no idea when to start placing pieces somewhere completely different and where I should do it, and I just run out of energy and interest in playing the game after about a third of the board is filled.

Writing this, I see that the game has potential for me. But the wide open decision space is strangely unappealing to me. I like more structure.

RATING: 9
I am a beginner at Go slowly getting a deeper understanding. Its simpler than chess in that there are less rules but it appears to be just as deep. I've limited myself to playing in the 9x9 grid board to learn fundamentals. In the smaller board mistakes become apparent faster, as there is, literally, less room for error! Games are played to their conclusion faster, allowing the beginning player more opportunities to try again and learn. Another option for teaching is to play first capture go, were the first player to capture a opponent's piece is the winner. Chess isn't nearly this easy to teach.

RATING: 8.3
Go is a difficult game to rate because it is a game that you basically get out of it what you put in.  To fully enjoy and appreicate the game it does involve some study to get to the point where you are not just placing stones randomly and that your moves have purpose. RATING: 10
Go is an amazing abstract game that has thousand of history behind it. You simply cannot approach Go as you would for a traditional game, time must be invested to learn about the basics in order to enjoy the game. Go has unlimited replayability and it is very difficult to master. The fun in Go is not just playing piece and beating your opponent, but rather it is about critically thinking about the whole board and each of your moves to outmanoeuvre your opponent's traps and strategy.

RATING: 6
My interest in Go was first piqued by the philosophers Gilles Deleuze and Felix Guattari, who wrote about it in A Thousand Plateaus. Years later, I took the time to learn it and notched up a number of games online. It took me dozens and dozens of attempts before I was able to win. I then set about making my own homemade copy of the game, before realising that it's not really for me. I'm not left-brained enough for such a dry and abstract game. I still have massive respect for Go and am glad I was able to gain an appreciation for it (if nothing else than as an intellectual exercise), but I doubt I'll ever play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Told to me by a Korean player many times better than I am:
(1)  "You need to build more house".
(2)  "Don't try to take everything.  Take more than half."

More practical advice (or perhaps less practical advice) for those looking to try go:

(1)  Steer clear of beginner sets that have pieces the size of M&Ms.  The awkwardness of those detracts from enjoyability.

(2)  It is the custom to play this game briskly and is considered impolite to dawdle extensively when making a move.  That isn't to say not to think things through, but don't get hung up.

(3)  It is also the custom to resign when faced with a certain loss, except in a teaching game or when both players agree to play it out for fun or learning.  This is a good practice and should be extended to other two-player games.

(4)  Ing's counting method is great.  That doesn't mean I use it.

RATING: 10
I love this game. I'm not a good player by any means, but it's SO much deeper than chess that comparing them it's not even fun. As Trevanian wrote, "Go appeals to the philosopher in any man and Chess to the merchant in him." Feeling is as important as reason in this game.

Go is the most beautiful and elegant game ever. The flow of the game, the shapes of the stones in the board, the way everything makes sense when you begin to get better... The way a move is not just good or bad, but beautiful in itself.

Go has just the perfect balance between strategy and tactics, between small-scale thinking and whole-board situations, attack and defense.

I have two sets: A bigger one (http://boardgamegeek.com/image/1246597/blackleadalchemist) and a small travel magnetic set (http://img04.taobaocdn.com/bao/uploaded/i4/T1ADjvXaNqXXctPQ31_041829.jpg)

RATING: 6
The difference between a game like Go and Chess is that while Chess is unintuitive, the mechanics of Go are the opposite: surround a piece and it falls. And yet the refinery of method possible makes a huge distinction between good and bad players. Which means you have a steep learning curve and a long way to something akin to mastery. This is the same problem as Chess and yet the elegance of the rules here makes me like Go so much more (even though I am a very poor player and rarely get to play).

The recent AI developments have somewhat killed this game for me. It has been revealed that good strategy is all about repeating the same patterns endlessly.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I think Go has some mystic qualities. It's a game of finding balance. Try to bite more than you can chew, and you lose. Play it too safe, you lose. It resembles the Yijing, Yin and Yang, heavy and light. I like how much intuition goes into playing this.

Funny how I was a bit scared of this game, it seemed like a huge commitment. But it's not, really. The learning curve is a bit steep in the beginning, but once you get over it it's plain fun, as long as you can find someone about your level to play with. And online, you can always do that. Even if you couldn't, there's handicaps, and they work well.

If you're going to play online, I recommend online-go.com. I haven't tried many other websites, nor want to. Know the difference between live (short) games and correspondence (long) games.

I followed an advice from someone here in the Geek, to take it slow, start on the smaller boards and slowly go up. I established a rule that I had to play 25 games on the 9x9 board, then more 25 on the 13x13 board, before I could play the full game. Now, that was a silly thing to do. I think a handful of games on each of the smaller boards will do. Better than setting a fixed number: feel your way.
EDIT: Actually, I did do a lot of tsumego before I started playing. If you don't do that, then it may be really a good idea to take it slow.

RATING: 9
Much like with chess, what can I say that hasn't already been said about this game? Where Chess is a combat/political metaphor, Go is a metaphor for life and death. It transcends the more human theme of chess. Watching a high level Go game is like watching an artist paint on a canvass. Chess can be like this as well, but I'm going to stop comparing them on this note: I love chess, but I can't help but admit that Go is the better game. It might just be the singlemost simple, yet incomprehensibly deep games to ever be produced by mankind.

VERDICT:

Regardless of my numerical score, I'm pretty sure this is the best board game ever invented (even if I'm terrible at it!).

9/10

RATING: 4
Go is a very simple but deep game, and I definitely understand why it’s so popular and have survived and been played for thousands of years! However, my rating is a personal reflection (like all of my ratings) of the fact that I simply don’t want to play this game much. It’s a nice abstract and all, but I don’t really fancy abstracts, and those I like tend to be shorter. I don’t want to overanalyze every move, and I think many abstracts can lead to this problem as there isn’t any randomness inherent in the game, so players think a little longer about what to do. I’m a big opponent of analysis paralysis and I don’t want to spend too much time playing an abstract game. And with Go’s 19 x 19 grid there is a LOT to think about and plan ahead after the first handful of stones have been set. So, while Go is a good abstract, it’s just not particularly enjoyable for me. I’ve also played Capture Go which I would rate higher as it’s tenser and shorter.

RATING: 7
A most fascinating ancient (in the range of 4000 years!) territorial game. You can  learn the rules in a couple of minutes, but you can play it all your life and never master it. Computers are known to have serious trouble beating human opponents, even relatively weak club players, making it a popular game in AI contexts. 

In spite of being a great game it doesn't really fit me as much as Shogi (Japanese Chess) or Xiangqi (Chinese Chess), so I don't play it much anymore. 

It is no overstatement to say that it should definitely be tried at least once by anyone who is serious about board games.

RATING: 8
I'm still learning the strategies, but it's a brain-burner that's worth the time and effort. I find myself mostly playing online, as there aren't many opponents in my area.

January 15, 2010 -- As of last night, I have played 55 games of Go. Many have been online or against an AI, but I've turned one friend into a fan of the game. We've played a few games when we have the chance, but we're both still learning the strategy and tactics of the game.

It's true what they say about Go. It only takes a few minutes to learn, but mastery could take a lifetime. I wish I'd started playing as a kid instead of learning as a thirtysomething. While I'm improving slowly, many strategic elements and patterns still elude me. However, I'm seeing where I have improved now that I'm playing against someone brand new to the game. I can see flaws in his moves that I have already overcome.

I still quite enjoy Go, and I think I'll get a chance to play more often now. No change in rating. 8/10

RATING: N/A
 This game is amazing. Simple rules but great depth: the epitome of a great board game. Stategy, tactics, psychology - this game has it all. 

If you become bored with most games after a few plays, then try Go. It's a game you may enjoy for the rest of your life.

Games of Go are often intense. For ancient Chinese, Go was a path to self-improvement. It's a martial art in board game form. 

Go can definitely be enjoyed at the beginner level. However, unlike many games, Go is not a game you can just pick up and play well after a couple plays. It requires patience and determination, and some initial frustration is common. It takes experience (and/or study) to recognize the implications of the simple rule set. I think some people don't give the game enough of a chance. 

My recommendations:

- Start on a 9x9 board. 
- Read the basic rules, such as these: http://www.usgo.org/usa/waytogo/W2Go8x11.pdf. 
- Take this tutorial: http://playgo.to/interactive
- Get a book like "Go for Beginners" by Kaoru Iwamoto and learn some basic strategies and tactics. 
- Play lots of games, make lots of mistakes, and learn from them: Download "igowin" and play against the computer, or play live in person, or play on the internet at http://kgs.kiseido.com (real-time) or http://www.littlegolem.net (turn-based).

RATING: 8
The undisputed king of abstract games, unsurpassed in terms of depth and complexity. Initially I thought the game's depth arose because of the large amount of possible moves you can make, but in actual games you're actually quite confined. (Read: most moves don’t make a lot of sense.) No, the complexity sets in because of the fact that at some point you’ll actually be playing several games of Mini-go on the board, attempting to make up for loss in one such minigame with winning in another. The game is therefore a very good example of complexity emerging from simple rules, which is an intruiging concept to build a game around. It makes for lots of longevity, which is a Good Thing.

It is impossible to begin playing Go without immediately running headlong into a rules issue dealing with a crucial aspect of the game: the end, and determining the score. I wracked my head about this for many hours until I finally got some sense of what was happening and why, and could explain it in terms I understand. I was told that the more popular Japanese rules using their quicker territory scoring (prisoners + empty surrounded area) would 'punish' you for playing into your own territory—and they do, of course. Not something you'd like to hear as a beginner. Then the 'easier' Chinese rules seem more appealing: no such penalty! However, this misses an important point that the two methods of scoring are [i]equivalent[/i], and nearly always yield the same point [i]difference[/i]. So if the Japanese rules are 'unforgiving', then so are the Chinese. [i]The[/i] point is—and remember this if you're starting out—is that the Japanese rules use a twisted definition of the emergent game properties of life and death to determine a group's viability, and thus whether territory belongs to one player or the other. It also needs to introduce rules about the handling of neutral territory. For beginners this means opening a can of worms because the procedure for determining life and death is quirky to say the least. There are of course many 'obvious' cases, but once you get into corner territory things get hazy and weird, and that's simply overasking. Far simpler is to simply let such emergent properties and neutral points be and just let the players decide for themselves, restricting matters to territory which is [i]sure[/i] to be under a player's control. If the players make mistakes here (for example by finishing prematurely when there is still territory to be won) they will only know by playing against someone else who is aware of these finesses anyway. What then remains is the difference in counting: the Chinese way—area scoring—ignores [i]dame[/i], and counts stones + empty surrounded territory. This is more cumbersome, but has the undeniable advantage that a player with a strong board presence will also have a large (and likely winning) score. Once you realise all that [i]then[/i] Go can be played with very simple and very straighforward rules, turning it into the sleek monster computers still have problems with. The rest is just window dressing to introduce some finer granularity to make up for first player advantage, handle the [i]dame[/i], do something about passing, and so forth. Interesting when skill level begins to amount to something (and then only in limited fashion), but not before.

I like this game, although I was unpleasantly surprised by my girlfriend who found it merely ‘ok’ even when played on the 9 × 9 board. After a bit of discussion it transpired that she found the game to be unpleasantly abstract in the sense that in her opinion I beat her too easily. She likes games with (reverse) psychology and intuition better because it allows her to push my buttons and in the process give her a better chance of winning. I then introduced her to the concept of handicaps, and this improved matters considerably to the point where she could win; afterwards she stated she wouldn’t say no to a few more rematches. Progress indeed!

Go is, for the moment, on a low burn. I have a fairly strong club in the vicinity which I should attend in order to make some progress beyond the 9 × 9 games I’ve been playing to date. But for now I lack the time for serious study. It is my hope that one day I can remedy that situation, as I think I would like the full game very much indeed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Go broke my heart, because I SO wanted to like it.  In the end, I had to accept the fact that it simply doesn't do it for me.  I want more drama in a game.  I want decisive actions, crushing blows.  Go is very subtle and indirect.  It feels like trying to put out a forest fire with water balloons.

I can still appreciate the game on some level, and have the utmost respect (and envy) for those who do enjoy it, but it's simply not for me.

I expressed my feelings best in [listitem=1977414] this geeklist entry.[/listitem]

UPDATE: I must have Stockholm Syndrome, because even though I dislike the game, even after swearing it off forever, I just can't stay away.  Raised the score a point accordingly.

UPDATE: Okay, we're officially back together.  Giving it another go.  For the childrens' sake.

RATING: 9
I need to get back into this.

Burns the brain pretty intensely when learning, and there seems to be more and more layers to the game as the learning progresses.

The last time I got into this it finally clicked that influence can be used as a staging ground for moves that are dual-purposed with regard to strategy, whereas I'd previous only played dual-purpose moves with regard to tactics.

Pushing through a book on tesuji certainly helped my reading and gave me the confidence to approach the game at the higher level, knowing I could reach an equitable outcome regardless of which strategy I had to adapt to.

RATING: 10
2009-05-05

At the lowest level, simply making sure that your pieces aren't needlessly taken - watching out for what is in Atari - is a challenge.

Then you start intuitively learning about sente.

Threatening opponent's pieces, recognising the patterns that can make life, connect or defy invasion.

There's a constant feeling of insight as within each game, surprises abound, mistakes happen and lessons are learned.

The handicap system keeps games exciting between players of a widely disparate level.

RATING: 8
Has a very different feel than any other abstract I've played. Stones are played by geometrical intuition as often as they're played by tit-for-tat calculation. I find this to make for an equally deep, but less stressful game, that nevertheless builds in excitement and has as much of a "story" as themed euros.

My wife and I started by playing 20-minute 9x9 games, and have moved up to hour-long 13x13. The flavor does change significantly as the board gets larger, and multiple areas play separately, then slowly coalesce.

RATING: 9
There is a reason this game has been played thousands of years.

One of the oldest games in the world.  Even though it is an ancient game, it is still played the same way (for over a thousand years at least).  It has simple components, almost no setup, and pretty simple rules that can be picked up quickly.

That is where the simplicity ends.  The more you play, the more you learn, and the more complex and fascinating the game becomes.

There is nothing new and exciting about Go.  It is only a perfect game that has been played by our ancestors, and that will be played by our decendents.  

I only rate this a "9" because I haven't played it enough.

RATING: 7
[2014 July 06; 12 plays; 8/10]
This is a astonishing game! I feel this game is deserving of a 10/10 rating, but not necessarily from me; it's a game that gets better the better you get at it. I think this is a game I'd like to play and understand more, but as simple as it is, it's very intimidating for new players--they can tell that it's incredibly deep the moment you start playing with them. Because of this, I have a hard time find people to play with and I feel "forced" to rate it an 8/10.

I find the game even more enjoyable with high quality stones and board, but I have probably the worst board and stones on the planet: thin fiberboard with uneven lines and thin melamine plastic stones. Once I have more plays I'll likely buy something slightly more lavish (hopefully a shin-kaya board and yunzi stones).

RATING: 9
Despite my reservations against 2p combinatorial abstracts, Go is the deepest and most elegant of its kind (perhaps of all games). It contains fuzzy positional judgement, non-local effects, pattern recognition, long term consequences and strategy in a genre usually about tactics and lookahead. This allows intuitive play by humans to succeed spectacularly against brute force AI calculation.

Go even inspired a [url=http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Combinatorial_game_theory]new area of mathematics[/url].

The thing I enjoy most about Go is its depth, having survived intense scrutiny by dedicated players and organisations around the world over thousands of years. I can keep learning significant new things, discovering how to get better, and making progress. Despite a dozen books, hundreds of games, and many tangible improvements to my game, I'm still baffled by "obvious" moves.

I also enjoy the indirectness of the game: assaulting from a distance and defending by making contact, attacking to profit rather than kill, giving up territory for "influence", nurturing the opportunities to make threats, ensuring your formations have "good shape".

Check out this fantastic post [article=5784316]talking about theme in Go[/article].

I have some beautiful marble stones but am looking for a wooden board for them.

I am still playing on an old inherited travel board that was given to my family when I was a child by friends who were moving on to a better quality board. That was over thirty years ago and I'm still working on figuring out the game.

RATING: 9
One of the skills all samurai were expected to cultivate, Go is one of the best 2 player strategy games. Very easy to learn, but complex enough to suit many levels of play. The nature of the board also means that you can use a limited area for faster games - I personally tend to play 11 by 11 rather than the full sized 19 by 19. Due to its popularity it&#039;s also very easy to find opponents on the internet.
The only real problem I have is that due its highly cerebal nature it&#039;s not the kind of thing you can whip out at parties. Especially at the more casual levels a small difference in ability will lead to a huge difference in score, and the lack of any element of chance means the contests can become very one-sided.

RATING: 9
Along with Chess, the best classic abstract, brain melting game on the planet. After having read Sun Tzu's Art of War in college, I had to investigate 'Go'. It remains an enigma to me, as it's much more difficult to plan out a strategy than it is in games like Chess. However, it is fascinating. If one has any interest in military history, or theory, then it's worth checking out, since it's all about surrounding & capturing territory (and pieces), while using the fewest pieces to do so. 

Not to compare, but one can get a good grip on Chess after playing for a few years, but with 'Go', it could take a lifetime. The mechanics are very simple to teach but the strategy that unfolds can be mind boggling- but in a good & interesting way. It's also a site to see a Go board filled with black & white pieces vying for control of space.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.4
There are 4 things I look at when I rank a game and the game of Go is king on 3 of the 4 standards.  These 4 things are:

1.  More strategy and less luck - Go had no luck involved.

2.  Not easily mastered - After playing even the most complicated games serveral times, you start to get a hang on what you need to do in the game to win.  After that time comes, the game can start to get boring and you you move onto other games.  The more I play Go, the more I realize how far I have to go before mastering the game so it never gets boring and I am always challenged.  This is a game I will be playing when I am old and in a wheelchair and there are not many games I can say that about.

3.  History - There is not a game with more history than this one.  There are books written about its history.

4.  Good Theme - This is one catagory that Go does not have and the only reason it is not a 10 in my ranking.

RATING: 10
Started playing in early 2007. My desert island game. Reaching 1 dan is a life ambition (I'm stuck on about 7 kyu at the moment). No other game has triggered a life ambition in me.

I used to love doing (and creating) abstract puzzles as a kid, and that desire for intellectual challenge is fulfilled in adulthood by go. I love how much of a role intuition plays.

Aesthetics are important to me in a game, and this one has the purest. It also has a ridiculously simple ruleset with deliciously complex consequences. With its lessons of balance, shape, initiative, sacrifice, and so on, this is the only game where I feel like getting better at it will actually make me a better person.

A life entirely devoted to this one game, and nothing else, would not be wasted.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
As a life-long chess player, I'm fascinated by Go's ability to be simultaneously simpler, and more complex than Chess. In Chess, the goal is to kill the opposing king at all costs. In Go, the goal is to maximize your liberty, and warriors lost are counted against you. In Chess, pawns are inconsequential at the end of the game. In Go, every unit counts. Chess is small enough to be nearly solvable by modern computers. In Go, the problem set is so much larger that Go AI projects have much more catching up to do. 

Looking at the differences between Chess and Go sortof represent the differences between stereotypes of Western and Eastern thinking to me.

And for that, I find it fascinating.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is a difficult game to rate because I know if it was the only game I ever played, I would probably really like it.  However, Go just isn't very much fun for the amount of time you have to put into it to really learn how to play the game well.  It suffers the same problem as chess in that a newbie will nearly always lose to an expert, meaning that it isn't a game you can just pull out once in a while or play with random people.  Maybe in a city with a big Go club or something I would get into this game.  It basically mirrors my thoughts on chess, only Go has even less theme and it even more difficult for a new player to get into.  If I was rating the game purely on its design or its appeal, perhaps it would be an 8 or 9, but since the rating is supposed to be a subjective judgment on how much I like a game and how likely I am to play it, Go gets a 5, and thats pretty generous.

There are a number of eurogames out there with go-like elements and playstyles.  I recommend through the desert.

RATING: 6
My father taught me Go at an early age. Even with a nine-stone handicap, he would trounce me. Although it is undoubtedly one of the greatest and most significant games ever, it doesn't "do it" for me in terms of board gaming fun. It is up there with Chess, Shogi, Backgammon, and other "classic" games, but all require substantial study, time, effort, practice, and many, many plays to become really good. I guess I play board games to have fun in terms of social interaction. Go is more like a wrestling match--kill or be killed; it's exhausting. I only comment on it, due to its nostalgia factor.

RATING: 10
I consider Go to be the perfect board game.

Go is zero luck, 100% strategy. It is a genius level of complexity distilled into something you can learn to play in ten minutes and spend 60 years perfecting.

Go is a game about life and death. The primary goal is to gain points by placing stones of your color on a large grid to surround empty regions of the board. However if either you or your opponent surround the other, those person's stones die and are removed permanently from the game. The entire rich complexity comes from both players trying to surround empty spaces while ensuring their own pieces are not surrounded by their opponent. It's deep, rich, and my all time favorite board game.

[u]UPDATE:[/u]Damn this game. I read the rules AGAIN and have played again and I still don't fully grasp all the strategy in this game, one of the deepest games I have played for sure and maybe period. 9 x 9 grid doesn't take too long versus the bigger one, especially if you get beat on. More plays and strategy articles are in order for this, I really need someone to teach me FTF then I know I will catch on to the deeper subtleties...

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Okay, thanks to a friend [i]trying[/i] to explain things (he is Chinese so things were getting lost in translation...) but mostly thanks to my new Android phone, I finally feel like I can play a pretty good/competent game of this (the phone program is awesome because it shows you territory control after every move which is a HUGE help for learning and playing).

It really is not that hard to grasp and the rules are ridiculously simple, yet if you are not taught properly in the beginning or try to teach yourself online you will likely be lost (didn't help me that I was likely switching to Chinese rules some games without knowing...DOH!)  

The game is a marvel of design truly in that such simplicity breeds such depth, a beauty to behold and will likely become a 9.5 or even 10 for me. I am not fully convinced yet that it has tons of different strategies to explore (of course I 'think' [never researched it myself just heard] that is pretty much a well known fact and don't doubt it, I just want to see some of them for myself) but then I am only 40 games in at this point. It just seems many of my games are taking on the same patterns time and again and I'm not sure I see that part of it changing. This is not to say I am a good player and have it all figured out, far from it. Pattern recongnition is not a big strength for me and I know my game needs tons of improvement, the AI still kicks my butt most times and only on level 5 out of 10, its not hard to see once you grasp the basics and know your way around why this is called a lifetime game, its incredibly deep and just plain tricky to "see" the true meaning (ramifications) of a single stone placement most times. Of course too, thinking about it more, I am truly playing mostly tactically and I probably need someone to show me a few strategy tips and playing a different person might really shake things up too (or just play more in general, the slower more painful way...) 

Anyway the game is a war, a brutal war of territory and a knife fight in an alleyway, the true Grandaddy of all Area Control games and the purest game I have ever played. If you have played Through the Desert the Euro game 2 player, this really is of a similiar flavour and you shouldn't be scared of it, the board is just much harder to "SEE" but it comes. The "EYE" rule was one of my stumbling blocks, once I fully "got it" (and its really simple) the rest just fell into place, again had someone taught me from the beginning things would have been SO much better for me than trying to learn on my own.  

At any rate I have a new love, I am not about to marry it and devote my life to it but when I can play an easy game against the AI on my phone in 20 - 30 minutes (13 x 13) it sure is a satisfying experience and I am thoroughly hooked at the moment. Now the next time my friend comes to visit I have a little 'surprise' waiting for him....  *-)  

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Okay, I have played another 20 games up to 60 now and while I have seen some good improvements in my play, I think its time I stop playing against a computer who can quickly calculate which of 100+ moves is the best before I throw my new phone against the wall. Increasingly frustrating yet so rewarding at times too. It also might seem that I am too aggressive at times, playing more offensive than defensive and I let certain "moves" bother me and will counter_counter_counter kinda cut off my nose to spite my face in a way and that likely loses me ground....

It seems I am at a plateau now and in order to improve further I will really have to do a little reading (or have someone teach me a couple of tricks). Due to the need for this, I'm not sure I can give the game a 10 but that only is the only thing stopping me otherwise this is THE abstract game. Let's see what happens from here.

RATING: 8
I've played more games against an AI than I can count...  I *really* want to play this face-to-face.

Learning the rules was a bit confusing at first (how do freedoms/liberties work again? how do you score?) but after playing a few practice games, the rules just seemed to click...  And that is one of the biggest reasons I like the game: the clicks.  Playing a game of Go is a learning experience.  It seems every few games, I learn something new or I think I see some pattern.  I have a feeling this is due to game play being so instructive: over time you can more easily see when you made a "bad" move (or series of moves).

The game can easily be played by people with different skill levels through an excellent handicap system and end scoring mechanism.  Also, game time can be modified easily by changing the size of the board.

I wish more people played this over chess.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
== Simple definition.
Go is a perfect information, 2-player, abstract, area control game.

== More accurate definition.
Go is an insidiously addictive way of life, where the more you play the more often you find yourself working out "just one more puzzle" at 2:00 AM.

During the game, players try to surround more territory than their opponent. Captured stones, those which have no empty points adjacent to them or their group, count against your score.  Play continues until both players pass in succession.  Normal games are played on a 19x19 grid, but beginners typically use a smaller board.

Go is wildly popular in Korea, China, and Japan. You can watch professional matches, complete with color commentary, on TV in these countries.  In the U.S. we have to be content with reading reviews and replays of professional matches.


== What I like the most.

* Games between players of differing skills are still fun. Go has a built-in handicap system that works extremely well. The weaker player (always playing black) gets to place a number of stones on the board prior to the game. It's common to refer to one player as being "3 stones stronger" than another, for example.

* A Go game is a "conversation" between players that is spoken with the stones.  As you carve up the board, laying claim to "your" territory, there's a balance that must be maintained. You only need to win by 1 point (actually 0.5), and trying to win in a landslide is usually a strategic mistake that will backfire horribly.  Simply knowing who is ahead in territory during the game is difficult. "Counting" is the practice of figuring out who is ahead, which determines the basic strategy that must be taken.  If you're ahead, you want to end the game with simple moves.  If you're behind, you need to make something happen and act more aggressively.  If you're ahead, but you behave like you're behind, then you're more apt to make mistakes and fall victim to a counterattack.

* Go is aesthetically pleasing.  The board, the stones, the bowls, the sound of a piece being played, the look of the board as a game progresses -- all of these add up to a game that is austere yet gratifying to the senses. This balances nicely with the mental taxation of playing the game itself.

* Improving in skill is fun. It's said that to improve you need to do three main things: (1) play a lot, (2) review your games, and (3) study life-and-death puzzles. Obviously, I enjoy playing the game. I try to review all of my games, and usually find several glaring errors. It's especially helpful to have a stronger player review the game for you. Life-and-Death puzzles, among others, are fun little brain burners. Doing several each day makes seeing the life and/or death of a group during a game more instinctive.

* The time system. While not specific to Go, and not always used, the normal method of keeping time in a Go game is called Byo-yomi.  This is a system where there is an initial block of time and then a series of overtime periods of a certain length.  As long as you make your moves in less than the overtime duration, you will never run out of time. If you use up all of your overtime periods, you lose.

Example: Online, I typically play with 25:00 mimutes + 5x(30 seconds byo-yomi).  This means that once my 25 minutes is up, I have to make the rest of my moves in under 30 seconds each. Each time I pass over the 30 seconds, one of my 5 byo-yomi periods is used up. If I take 1:01 to make a move, then I lose two byo-yomi periods, etc. 


* No opening book moves. Not exactly anyway (certainly nothing to the degree of Chess). There are key strategic opening principles, although applying them correctly is a difficult task. There are also localized series of moves called "josekis." These are moves which result in a roughly equal outcome for both sides. Like book moves in Chess, these require both memorization and a deeper understanding of what's going on behind the moves so that varations don't wreck your game.

The conventional wisdom is that you should not spend a lot of time memorizing josekis, as it will actually make you a weaker player, until you're already strong enough to understand why the moves are joseki in the first place. It's OK to learn some josekis, but usually you want to do this in reaction to a position that happened in a game.  For example, if you keep losing your entire corner to an invasion on the 3-3 point versus your 4-4 point stone, you should take 15 minutes to review the basic josekis of this position and figure out where you're going wrong.


* The balance between strategy and tactics. These are highly intertwined in Go. It's easy to get caught up in the mentality of, "I must destroy my opponent's stones!" This is usually an error, however, and generally difficult to accomplish anyway. A better approach is, "I will threaten to destroy my opponents stones, but as he defends against it I'll gain territory or influence instead."  Attack for a reason, and not just because blowing up stones is fun (which it is). 

Given that you are attacking for a reason, which is typically strategic in nature, you still have to carry out the attack, which is generally tactical. How you conduct an attack, or a defense, can have an enormous impact on your overall strategy.



== Reference material

Go is a "lifetime" game which rewards study, practice, and creativity. 

There are also plenty of books to read for all skill levels, and other forms of instruction.  While harder to find in the U.S. than in Asia, there are still plenty of English language resources available.  Thanks to the Internet, those of us in the western world have access to a good selection of Go info.

* [url=http://senseis.xmp.net/]Sensei's Library[/url]: The English Go wiki.  Start with their introduction and beginner pages.

* [url=http://senseis.xmp.net/?LearnToPlayGoSeries]The Janice Kim Books[/url]: A series of introductory Go topics. Very readable, and well written. They'll get through the very basics of the game.

* [url=http://senseis.xmp.net/?TheSecondBookOfGo]The Second Book of Go[/url]: (The first book being the rules.) This is an overview of major areas of study.  It does not go in depth on any topic, but it goes deep enough.  A friend gave me his copy after I started playing, and it helped immensely. It fits nicely with the Kim books, especially if you've worked through the first two or three of hers already.

* [url=http://internetgoschool.com/audio.vhtml]Guo Juan's Audio Go Lectures[/url]: This is a series of lectures by [url=http://senseis.xmp.net/?GuoJuan]Guo Juan[/url] and other professionals who work with her on the project. The lessons are presented as a Go board with an audio track. Each lesson costs 1 euro and is available for 1 month after purchase. (Think of it like a 1 month rental.)  There is a [url=http://internetgoschool.com/studyguide.vhtml]study guide[/url] which will give you a track of lessons to work on. She definitely does not want people blowing through these lessons as fast as possible.  The intention is that you'll watch one several times over the course of a few weeks, and also play a lot of games during this time as well.

* Guo Juan also has [url=http://internetgoschool.com/groupclasses.vhtml]online group classes[/url] that run a few times per year. 135 Euro is real money, but for 8 weeks of lessons it's a bargain. I've attended several terms of her class over the years, and it's great. The only reason that I haven't done it more is that the U.S. and European students are placed together, which makes it challenging to find common times for class games.

* [url=http://ayd.yunguseng.com/]American Yunguseng Dojang (AYD)[/url] I'm currently a member of AYD. In-seong Hwang runs this school in parallel with his larger European school. He's an 8-dan amateur who was previously in the Korean pro school. He's also the official go instructor for Switzerland and France. I attended his lectures at the 2015 U.S. Go Congress and was blown away at the clarity and practicality of his lectures. Every evening after one of his lectures the playing room was buzzing about the techniques that he had demonstrated. Almost everything seemed like it could be applied in your very next game. At $330 per 3-month season, it's a serious investment, but I have not regretted it for a second.

The general class format is 1 lecture per week (most weeks), and 1 class game per week with an extra weekend game thrown in once per month. Most games are reviewed the same night they are played, with the rest coming within a week or two. All reviews are in video format. He will teach to your level, but will not pull punches. The goal is improvement, not inflating your ego. If you make a good move, he tells you. If you make a bad move, he tells you. If you make a terrible move that someone at your level has no business ever making, he lets you have it [url=https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eGKdle1bbvo]and you feel shame.[/url] The class is a joy. The other players are great, and the school is strong as hell. 

* [url=https://www.youtube.com/user/nicksibicky/videos]Nick Sibicky's videos.[/url] Nick teaches the kyu-level classes at the Seattle Go Center. Many of these classes have been uploaded to his Youtube channel. He's a medium strength dan amatuer and an excellent instructor. 

* [url=https://www.youtube.com/user/dwyrin/videos]Dwyrin (a.k.a. BattsGo)[/url] Dwyrin is a streamer who puts in marathon sessions on Twitch where he plays on Korean servers. He also puts together instructional videos and reviews. He's a high-level dan amateur.

* [url=http://senseis.xmp.net/?GoBooks]More books[/url]: A good wiki article to use as a starting point for finding the right books for your level and interest.

* [url=http://www.gokgs.com/]Play online on KGS[/url]: This is an awesome internet server written by BGG's own [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/user/wmshub]William Shubert[/url]. I especially like the "auto match" feature, which lets you set up some basic game preferences and then matches you with other people who want to play the same type of game as you and who are roughly your same strength.

I play as [url=http://www.gokgs.com/gameArchives.jsp?user=bray]Bray[/url] on KGS.  Feel free to look me up and say hi.

== Learning to play

I've noticed a pattern from people who tell me that they hate Go. They all tried their first game on a 19x19 board with an impatient teacher. This is recipe for pure hatred, because the game will make zero sense. There is no reason at all to play on a full sized board at first. 

Start on a 9x9 board with a 4 or 5 stone handicap against someone who knows what he's doing, or against a program like [url=http://www.sente.ch/software/goban/]Goban[/url].

Don't worry about losing. It's going to happen a lot. You'll probably find that you're making the same tactical errors often. That's OK too. The learning curve on this game is steep. When you start out at a rank of 30-kyu, the road to 1-dan (a.k.a. shodan) is a long one. (I'm sitting at 4-kyu on KGS after ~1000 games. This is likely on the slow side, as there are people who have made 1-dan in fewer games. Take it as a rough value. I'm stubborn with my bad habits, so 1-dan is going to be a longer road for me.) Even though most players will never reach shodan, that rank doesn't mean "expert." It means that you know the basics and now you're ready to start learning how to play the game for real. Obviously the word "basic" takes on a whole new meaning in a game of this depth.


== Instruction

You've played your first 100 games and have some small clue of what's going on. The best way to improve is to have an instructor and review your games. There are several levels to this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The board consists of a 19x19 grid. There are stones in 2 colors - 181 black stones and 180 white stones. Players take turns placing stones at intersections on the grid in an attempt to surround and capture opposing stones and wall off "territories" on the board that score at the game's end. Because you are always trying to capture stones and secure key points on the board to define your territories - and trying to prevent your opponent from doing the same - there is a fine degree of both offensive and defensive thinking that is needed on every turn. As stone placement flows across the large grid an impressive variety of situations develop. Not only do you have to think both offensively and defensively, you have to react tactically and plan strategically as well. The capture element is fought in localized situations that develop, while you always have in the back of your mind ways to secure territories for the game's end. A rich and rewarding game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I think that this is the best strategy game of all time.  

It has a great amount of depth and keeps me interesting and reading about strategy.  I find it more fun to play than chess.
This game rewards the skill of the player more than most games on this list, so will put a lot of people off.

It keeps teaching me about how I should play other games.  The most impotent lesson that I have learned is that theme doesn't matter.  What matters is how the game plays.  That is what will keep you coming back for more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The best strategy game of them all. This is the only game that I have wanted to study to more depth than any other. It is amazing considering how simple the rules are.  The strategy and depth is staggering. No other game can't compete.  This game proves that theme and mechanics really aren't always that impotent to a great strategy game.  If you invest time into this game, you will learn also more deeply about yourself and life.  Only 3 problems with this game;
 1. None of the board gamers I know play or want to play (not trendy I guess).
 2. You have to learn some basic strategy or you won't get far.  This normally means reading at least one book.  
 3. it is an abstract and puts people off.  For example the dice tower never mention abstract games in there top 10 lists.
These are not problems for me, so it gets a 10. This games is number one on my list.

RATING: 8
Go is an amazing game: any way you look at it, the games rules are simple, and can be taught in a minute.  But that's where it starts.

The game's depth is expressed in its persistent existence of 4000 years.

People say, I'd rather not devote my time to just one game, but many, that's why I don't play GO (or Advanced Squad leader for example), but if that game is superior, and has an actual lasting quality, then I say "WHY NOT!"  By no means am I saying this is the only game I'm going to play, but I am saying 40 years down the road, of my current games collection, this will probably be one of the few played due to its sustainability of depth and simplicity (and poor vision and arthritis).

Plus, wouldn't it be cool to be able to say "I'm a 4th Dan."  Yes, this game has actual ranks.

RATING: 7
I will never be good at Go. I will never be mediocre at Go. I can play it, I can hold my own against a very weak opponent, but beyond that - I just have to accept that it is not something I have the time to master.

Aside from that - this should rank as the greatest abstract game of all time. Far more elegant than chess, in the simplicity of its rules, yet quite a bit deeper in terms of the brain-power required to succeed. One of the few games, along with chess and perhaps Cathedral, for which owning an expensive set is worthwhile, for the artistic value alone even if never played.

RATING: 7
Why I like Go:
-Simple Rules, can be explained in under a minute
-Good Replayability- this won't be a game you play a couple times and never touch again, unless the game doesn't turn out to be your cup of tea, you will find yourself taking this game out of its box every once in a while.
-Packed with Decisions- this game will have you constantly analyzing the board and trying to come up with the right move
-Versatile Equipment-the go set can be used for multiple different games, such as my favorite, Connect6.
-Elegant- hard to explain but after playing most would agree with me

Verdict:
Definitely worth trying out on a free app/website. And if you like it, I would recommend buying the physical version as you can play other games on a Go set and because of the enhanced experience of playing in real life.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Pick a stone, place it and try to enclose as much territory as you can. That are the rules of Go without entering in special situations or scoring. The rules are terribly simple to teach, but this simplicity hides an incredibly complex and taxing game.

Are you looking for a brain burner? Don't mind if it's an abstract or, even more, you prefer it? Then you must play Go. Magnificent strategy game with an extremely complex structure. Once you have give it a try you might like it or not, but isn't difficult to understand why this one has been alive for so many years.

The problem with this game is the same that chess, shogi and all those classic heavy games: it has been overstudied, so if you don't find opponents of your level one player will consistently trample the other.

Rating: :star::star::star::star::nostar:

[i]For more information on how I rate games, [geekurl=/user/Kodeir]go to my profile[/geekurl][/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Fascinating abstract strategy game for two players. Simply placing stones on a board. One by one. Evolving into a display of pure elegance, amazing depth and sheer beauty. ***** The handicap stones allow opponents of unequal experience/skill to play Go in a more balanced way, which is rare for abstract games. ***** A 9x9 board is ideal to learn the look and feel of the game (Igowin), but playing on the standard 19x19 board is of course much more fun (Many Faces of Go). And nothing beats playing against real opponents! ***** After playing around with the pc games, a few sessions in the local Go club end of 2005 and reading books for more than two years, I finally joined KGS on 20th May 2008 ... and got really hooked. (Note: only KGS plays against real players are logged in my games played - not those against bots; Well, I actually played only once against a bot on KGS - why bother with bots when you can play with real people?) ***** KGS: 22/05/2008: 16kyu; 26/05/2008: 15kyu; 29/05/2008: 14kyu; 23/06/2008: 13kyu; 16/07/2008: 12kyu; 09/09/2008: 11kyu.

RATING: 8
Not a light board game really, it does take a few hours to actually "get" that your groups die unless they have 2 real eyes, and then some practice games to see them come into being on the board. The ko rule might take 10 games to understand it. 

Played it a few times on an app or on the pc, read some books about it and played a few times with my GF. In the end I find it too intense, and playing against a better player feels like everything you do keeps you slipping off.

It is more elegant than chess or draughts (the 10x10) version in the sense that there is no "draw" (remise), and it allows for some handicap.

-1 for the learning curve
-1 for the 19x19 and 13x13 games take too long, and there is nothing to do while the opponent is pondering his move.

RATING: N/A
When I really begin to dig into this game, I expect that it may surpass chess in its intrigue, organic beauty, and cosmic significance. A case has been made that this game actually facilitates the evolution of human consciousness, and I see this to be likely. A culture that plays Go is truly a culture to be respected. The game appears sublime in its capacity to embody certain characteristics, patterns, or truths of the universe, while being both infinite in scope and deeply enriching to those who embrace it. To excel at Go requires one to throw off the confines of desire and ego, to exist in a state of mental and spiritual balance and harmony. Go is therefore more than a game—it is a life teacher, ineffable in the rich and rewarding depths that spring from its simplest essence—and as a board game, it appears singular in its tendency to guide players along paths to awareness and understanding.

RATING: 10
A venerable abstract strategy game, with such depth and complexity that it is daunting. The simplicity of the rules, together with the amazing range of strategic depth, make this game almost a perfect abstract strategy game. It can be played on varying sized boards for varying challenges, from a 7x7 board for a quick tactical game to a 19x19 board for an hours long strategic game, with plenty of tactical conflicts during the course of the game.

RATING: N/A
I used to have dreams about this game.  You couldn't imagine a simpler game -- all of its rules could be written on a 3 x 5 index card.  You also couldn't imagine one that's harder to master -- the best computers can't touch even a just-competent go player.  Go has a kind of flow and logic to it that Chess doesn't have, and its suprises and swings of fortune can be even more brutal.  This is one of the only games that I could see studying for the rest of my life.  I only wish I knew more people who liked it so I could play it more.

UPDATE:  10 ---> UNRATED.  My thoughts on Go's infinite depth remain unchanged.  In the years since I wrote the above, what's remarkable is not how little I've played Go, but how little I've missed it.  I recently moved somewhere that's not a mile from the local metropolitan Go center, and I haven't even brought myself to stop by.  

This is a function of my changing tastes in games.  I'm less interested in purely spacial games, and when I want to play one, I reach for a game that concludes within half an hour instead of Go's 2 - 3 hours.

I no longer kid myself that I have the inclination to study Go seriously.  And it's not a game that I fancy playing casually.  So I have consciously decided not to play Go at all.

And so here we have the dilemma of a game that I regard as infinite (suggesting a 10 rating) and yet one that I have consciously decided [b]not to play[/b] (suggesting a 1).  I don't know how to reconcile that difference.  So I will remove any rating at all.

RATING: 9
This is the most elegant game of all time.  Only two rules, and a wealth of strategy.  They still haven't made a Go AI that can beat the world master.  This is decades after they made a chess AI that could beat the world master.  

And this is an abstract strategy game that I am frequently able to interest non-game-geeks into.  That's a lot more than I can say for many of the other great abstract strategy games.

I built a custom board made out of stone.  It's the best!  Good luck finding that anywhere on the Internet though.  That's why I made it.

Actually, here's the only reason I'm not rating Go a 10/10.  Component quality.  In most board games, there's a definite publisher, and you rate the board game as a whole experience from that publisher, including the component quality.  With Go, there's no original publisher, and a million companies make it.  

Here's the problem: none of these dim-witted companies have decided to make a Go board that is actually awesome.  Everywhere you look, it's just the same bamboo-looking thin board and plain, plain stones.  No one is selling stone Go boards.  Chess has lots of manufacturers making really great chess sets.  Go needs this.

Now, is the lack of awesome component quality due to whoever invented the game?  Clearly not.  But I am deducting that from its score nonetheless, because I believe all scores should be from the end user's perspective.

9/10.

RATING: 10
Go is a game with very simple and elegant rules (pretty abstract though), but with dazzling depth. As a matter of fact, I didn't like my first 3 games (about ten years ago). Therefore, I didn't want to play it for more than a year after those games. After a lot of begging by a fellow go player I tried a 4th game. However, after that game I was sold. This gem is far more strategical than chess, and allows for more freedom of style. Tactics have to be embedded into a grand scheme strategy to be really effective. Opening theory is way less important than it is for chess, which I consider a huge advantage. Go also has a very sophisticated handicap system allowing for more challenging games between players of different strength.

RATING: N/A
If you're just picking up this game at the behest of someone who plays it regularly, be prepared to lose. A lot. The rules are simple, yet this game arguably offers the deepest strategy of any ever devised. Intuition and pattern recognition are key, but acquiring these is not easy as there are a LOT of patterns to recognize, and intuition must be honed through long experience. A game of Go on a full-size board can last hours. Starter boards offer faster games and slightly shallower strategy for beginners.

RATING: 10
Beautiful and tense.  Every stone placement is a subtle maneuver in an elaborate struggle.  The ultimate game, as far as I'm concerned.  I'm not that strong of a player, but you don't have to be a great player to have fun and appreciate the potential of the game.  For those out there who are intimidated by their first experience, I'd urge a few more plays to see if it clicks, preferably with another beginner who is enthusiastic about learning the game.  End-of-game plays and scoring can be somewhat intimidating for newer players, but remember the adage: "When in doubt, play it out!"

RATING: 9.5
Go is such a beautiful and brilliant game. I have nothing but the utmost respect for it - the few full games I've had the pleasure of playing have challenged me in ways I hadn't thought possible from a board game.

But herein lies my complaint. Go is so huge and so complex that it requires an enormous time commitment. I'm talking 7-8 hours for one game. As the board becomes more populated, and piece placement carries greater consequence, you find yourself taking more and more time before placing a piece. I just don't have that kind of time to devote to a game regularly. 

So, as much as I love Go, I can only enjoy it every once in a while. And that's a shame because it takes regular practice to develop any sort of measurable skill for this game.

The game is simple, like chess, but brilliant.  The only difficulty, is like chess you have to study to get better.  The game does require an immense amount of time to master.  

Every true gamer ought to have played this a few times though.

RATING: 7
Hardest game I ever played! Boils my brains out!

It has a somewhat steep learning curve - you can get discouraged if you start on too big a goban, and for the first few games you feel like you dont know what you are doing.

When you get to know and understand the game better, it gets really exciting, it's a tight struggle between the players from the start to the very end!

I rate the game only a 7 because of the rating explanations on BGG. Id give it at least a 9, but I cant say I'd always play the game - it takes quite long, its a two player game and it really is difficult! Playing this game can be EXHAUSTING! I need to be in a mood for an extreme mental challenge to start a game of go. :)

RATING: 8
This game, more than any other, makes me wish more people were aware of it and that more people played it. It gets plenty of serious study, but this game is conspicuously absent when I check out my friends' board game collections. To be fair, a nice set can be cost-prohibitive, but like most board games, the grid and stones are easily repurposed for other games (see also: Gomoku, Conway's Game of Life, etc.)

Very rarely do I find someone who plays Go, and they are always utterly casual or utterly serious matches. There ought be more of a middle ground here... where is it?

RATING: 10
Insanely deep game --- both tactically and strategically --- as modern AIs have shown, despite relatively simple rules. Also playable by all ages; I am a decent club player (~1 dan level), and have played with a couple kids under 10 who are about the same ranking!

Some additional nice aspects:
1) Can scale board size (e.g. 9x9, 13x13, 19x19) depending on skill level and time constraints.
2) Very nice handicap system for matches between players of different strengths.

Only negative in my mind is that this game requires a great deal of study, and even then often makes the player feel dumb :) More seriously, this game is (excessively?) mentally draining.

RATING: 7
2 players
placement
capture

Computers are only just now starting to be able to beat humans at this. Used to treat senile dementia in Japan. Emperor of Tibet decided over a game. Nuking Hiroshima only caused a four hour delay of a title match. Complete, official Japanese records go back to the 1400s. The second largest wiki on the internet is devoted to this game.

There are really only 2-3 rules for placement. The game ends when one player resigns or when both players pass in succession. Scoring is simple.

RATING: 10
The game is so simple and elegant, there's just a few, straightforward mechanics - nothing extraneous at all - but emergent from this simplicity is an extremely complex, multi-level interaction on the board. Playing a lot starts to affect the way you perceive things around you, with Go-influenced patterns revealing themselves constantly. It demonstrates to me how stripped-down-to-fundamentals the game is; it's all about micro and macro-scale interactions. Reminds me of ecological processes. Very cool stuff and very mentally stimulating. I don't typically have 'favorites' - variety is the spice of life after all - but Go is one of the few instances where I could say if I had to, "This. This right here, I could have just this one game for the rest of my life and be content". Luckily, people don't put guns to your head for things like that.

RATING: 10
It's a classic, deep,abstract game of space,logistics and maneuver. Like Chess, it's as much a language as a game, and thus is best learned when very young. An interesting thing about Go to me is the way people who like it tend to take shots at Chess in comparison, while I've never heard Chess players take shots at Go. Of the two, I prefer Chess because it is less abstract and has a bit more 'personality', and I just prefer the simplicity and finality of the checkmate over Go's territory victory conditions. Both great games, and both great games to teach to kids because of the logic and spatial awareness required.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I can’t really contribute anything meaningful about Go that hasn’t been written before but this game has taken me by storm. After some dumb games against an AI on 6x6 I became intrigued - how could this simple game be so hard? Then I upped the ante and tried to play on a 9x9 board - which left me completely dumbfounded. Then I started a game on the normal sized (19x19) board and fled in horror at the wide open space before me. 

I’ve always enjoyed Chess but this is something else. This is a game that is built and shaped by the players, and so emerges vastly different every time. It offers exploration, and delightful discoveries to a degree Chess can't match. I know I'll never be good at this game but I love playing it all the same.


EDIT: A year after my flailing introduction I'm now a 1d KGS, I still don't know anything about the game though.

RATING: 9
Possibly the abstract I've been seeking. With competent play (on my part), I have little doubt this could be a 10. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Game in a Nutshell: Control the most area on the game board by taking turns placing a black or white stones. Surrounding an opponents group takes it off the board.

Two-player only.

Pros: For a pure abstract, it's interesting how the game almost feels organic and flows so nicely. Can be played anywhere with some rocks and a graph. It's a game where maybe you can learn against a better opponent in stages by just hoping to score a few more points every game and not worry so much if you're losing a ton in the process.

Cons: It might look simple, but it's far from intuitive to understand for its intricacies. A game with this big of a following means it's going to be taken a little more serious and harder to have a 'fun' match. Experience and pattern recognition will give players a significant advantage and make it hard to find adequate opponents to keep it interesting.

Other: Ok, so I've only played this for one day and am nearly boggled by the depth of the game that I can't even see. I had fun learning and can imagine in the long run this having the potential to be rated a 10. 

Best Use: When you want a deep, deep playing experience and simplicity puts a warm fuzzy in your heart.

RATING: 10
This game is a life saver! Having practically no social life whatever, and seeing as the friend of mine who practically lives at my house could play Monopoly on repeat from now till doomsday, I have to find a lot of solitary activities to occupy myself. Granted, Go is supposed to be 2 player. But I have never had so much fun playing any other board game, I think. I play this alone whenever I have the time, and I just love doing it. Plus, you can actually make your own set quite easily to try it, with paper (try it on a 9x9 point grid)and literally almost anything for pieces... candy, bottle caps... I used glass blobs from the craft store... ANYTHING. Then when you realize you can't live without this game, you will probably end up at Yutopian Enterprises buying their awesome plastic pieces, which I also cannot live without, and one of their cheap wooden boards, till you can work your way up to a "respectable" board with legs. Watch your pocketbooks... you can MAKE this game for next to nothing... but once you get thoroughly addicted, you can spend literally thousands of dollars getting the coveted "perfect set" of table and stones. I cannot fathom doing without this addiction! Visit Sensei's Library or some of the other cool websites out there to get yourself hooked. Just don't blame me when you start bringing your board to the breakfast table... (I did.)

RATING: 9
Although I initially gave it a 10, considering how I've decided to give ratings, basing them on considering every aspect, I have to take 1 point. 

The game is the best there is (and probably ever will be) when it comes to 2 player strategy game, it's elegant, and the contrast its simplicity makes with its depth it's fantastic. 

However, to get the most of it, you have to play it at a regular basis (unless you aquire a somewhat highish level). If you don't play for a period of time, you'll notice when you go back to it you're alamost back to square one; you won't be able to play as good and you'll remember that you were better and how it felt, and that'll probably bother you (or at least it happens to me, and I've known several people have experienced the same thing). 

Now don't get me wrong, that aspect of the game where you need to read about it and watch games to improve can actuallu be in itself a pleast experience in itself; but that means a good amount of time invested in the game, and so because you need to make such an invest to get that enjoyment I feel I can't give it a perfect score.

RATING: 9
Used bookstore find - a nice wooden set with slide out trays for the pieces. Still unplayed and sealed in plastic when I got it.

I've played some Go, and while I am not a huge fan of abstract brain-burners (I despise chess) I can appreciate this game for its incredible complexity, deeply rich strategy, and the extent to which, when I do play it, I am absorbed by it. Not a game I want to play every day, but when I am in the mood, I have to admit how excellent a time I have (with the right opponent.)

---------

No idea where my Go set went.

And I have recently attended a couple of Go-Club meetings, but was so utterly outclassed that it was almost an act of self-flagellation to play.

I guess here is my thinking on this: there are single books - like the Tao Te Ching, The Republic or The Symposium, Being and Time, Hamlet, The Brothers K., to name a few - that I could spend the rest of my life reading and re-reading only that one book, and be relatively happy. With each re-reading I am sure I would gain a new profound understanding, and still not plumb the depths of wisdom to be had. But why would I want to read only one book over and over for the rest of my life, when there are just so many other books to read?

And that is my feeling about Go in a nutshell. To play it the way it deserves to be played, I'd have to give up too much time and energy that I would otherwise spend playing other games. Maybe that's ok for some. But my time is limited. And I think the rich variety makes me happier.

Goodbye, Go. I think I am done with you.

---------------------------

Update: Somebody donated a cheap set to my classroom and I find myself playing this again, albeit with kids. This game is just about perfect for me if I can play it casually and not worry too much about who will win--just allow my mind to focus on the current skirmish I am involved in and be surprised by the outcome. As such, I will probably never be a very good player of this, not in the way it deserves, but I will certainly play it if offered.

RATING: 10
+2 has exactly 3 rules, yet is the most complex game imaginable

+2 mechanics of balance that reflect life: life and death, aggression and passivity, questions with no single answer 

+2 a meta-game that develops not from direct rules, but from the elegance of the game itself. 

+2 bustling community of avid players, and professional level play. 

-2 though the rules are very simple, as with many abstract boardgames,  the broader strategy is not accessible without a bit of work and study. 

-1 2 player only

A game that is beautiful in its simultaneous simplicity and complexity. I think every gamer should at least scratch the surface. Highly recommend Nick Sibicky (also a board game designer) and his series of Go lectures - he really brings the game to life.

RATING: 6
I think I like this game? It's certainly interesting. The rule book in my copy wasn't the easiest to follow with the rules exceptions that can crop up in certain situations, but overall, Go is most definitely a game that has deep strategy with very simple rules. I guess the only thing that I can say against it is that there are so many other games that have taken what it has done, and made it more modernized. Taking turns putting out one stone is a bit dull compared to games like Through the Desert, where you're able to put down multiple camels at a time. That being said, I do think that this is a game that deserves its recognition. Despite its age, this is one of the most tense, thoughtful, and interesting games out there. I'm not sure that I want to dive in to this, but I typically dislike abstracts, and this was a fun one.

RATING: 5
Go used to occupy the throne at the top of my games collection and give me hipster cred while allowing me to explain why it's oh so much better than Chess. But to me, it's actually akin to that book you never read while its presence on your shelves supposedly makes you look smart to visitors. The learning curve is extremely steep and, more than any other game, its very clean and simple ruleset does *not* make it easy to grasp, no matter how much I fantasize about how semi-competent I would get if I spent my evenings on KGS for a couple months.

Also people trying to lure people into Go tend to actively avoid talking about the final scoring phase for a while, because that part is quite daunting.
