RATING: 10
Simply the best game ever!
The one I would take to the deserted island... no wait... one of its beauties is: you could easily make it out of stones and shells :-)
The rules are pure and simple and easy to teach. At the same time the game has the deepest comlexity of any game I know.
Learning it is much more about learning to get a "feel" for it in the beginning, learn to know about good shapes, build up intuition etc. not the rote learning of openings that happen very fast when getting a bit deeper into chess for example.
Another beauty of go is that it has a superb handicap system. Experienced players can play beginners and both have a challenging gaming experience.
If you like two player abstracts, and haven't had a go (pun intended) - then by any means go for it!

RATING: 10
Simple rules, but a real brain burner.  It's easy to see why this is the ultimate classic.

I love the elegant beauty of the components and the gameplay never ceases to amaze me.  The more you play the more you realize there is to learn which always keeps the game fresh.

This game definitely replaces chess for me.  Chess just seems rather pedestrian by comparison.

The handicap system is brilliant, it keeps a new player from getting frustrated and makes the game extremely accessible.  The scalability of the differing sized boards also gives the game marvelous flexibility.

RATING: 6.2
about the highest rating I give any abstract game is what this fine game deserves. Amazing and complex yet intuitively simple. This game has stood the test of time for the right reasons and rather than focusing on thinking moves ahead or examining possible moves on a Micro level like Chess does, Go forces the player to think about area control and overall influence on a macro level. The game and rules are actually less elegant than many may remember. A good game that's held up but there are simply better games even if you only count purely abstract games. It also takes too long for what it is.

Fun facts: Since I was my club's VP, I couldnt participate in our tournament, but the next year, I came back as an alumni, then faced last year's winner and then last year's runner up back to back (the three of us were the best at the school at the time, and the seats werent seeded >_>) and I beat them both. It was probably my proudest moment in go. I didnt actually win the tournament though because in the finals, the other kid had to leave, and I felt like I had accomplished what I wanted (I also didnt want the starbucks gift card since I didnt drink the stuff), so we didnt play and I let him have the win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Vastly overrated (some people see the meaning of life in this game*), to me, it's OK. One problem is the position gets so complicated after the opening moves, even for beginners playing on a 9x9 board, that you need to be some sort of Zen master to be able to read it and play it from there. So unless you're one of those Asian Go prodigies that's been raised on Go since birth, you're just guessing most of the time. I blame games like this for the phobia against deep strategic games in the boardgame fandom currently. 

It's not really a dry game, for example, Ko fights are fun, but it can get boring. Most of the time it just feels like you're placing stones on the board just to place stones on the board.

Supposedly there's some sort of elaborate scoring system to decide who won after a game's over, but all that's just smoke and mirrors. In really both players just stare at the board for awhile after their game's over and come to an agreement on who won. Unless one player gets bored before the other one and resigns. 
 
So yeah, the goal of the game is basically to come to a stalemate.

It's been fun to follow the AlphaGo games though.

*I've heard this game called a "transcendent expression of humanity" before. Maybe it's just me, but that sure is some BS.

RATING: 6

Personally I find the game interesting and challenging. A great mental exercise. However the fun factor is low for me. It's best played against a similarly skilled opponent. I do find it more enjoyable than chess.

If you enjoy abstracts then you should be playing this game. This has been one of the best abstracts I've played so far.

RATING: 9.3
Ohhh mannn... You thought chess was hard? Get on my level, punk. The basic rules are even easier than chess. You just put down a little thing on the board anywhere you want* and that's it! Of course where you put it actually matters since there are 361 distinct places to put it. 

This game is probably the hardest, most balanced game ever**. As such, it's entirely dependent on skill. If you dislike losing without being able to blame it on bad luck, then DO NOT PLAY THIS GAME. It will crush you if you don't have a fighting spirit, and perhaps a slightly more than miniscule bit of masochism.

*Except into atari or maybe ko. It will take you roughly 200 hours of gameplay to get an intermediate understanding of ko.

**With more than 5 regular players.

RATING: 10
The perfect abstract game. Mock/Split/Flank Attack, Defence, Trap, Reconnaissance, Sacrifice, Retreat, Last Ditch Fight, Close Combat, Long Range Attack and much more is in this gem of a game. Brutal tactical possibilities, but normally the game is won by better insight in its extreme strategic deepness.

Don't become depressed when you start playing and get crushed like a hollow nut without even knowing what hit you. It takes some time to aquire the basic skills, but then Go will be fun to play no matter how good you are. Games between by players of large knowledge differences are possible due to the excellent handicap system. You get/give some handicap stones and both players can play an interesting and exciting game just for fun - it's already clear who is the better player.

If the goddess of gaming would grant me perfect insight in one game I wouldn't have to think a second which one to choose.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10

RATING: 10
A timeless game.  Seriously, Go was already floating around when existence began and it will be there when the last stars wink out of the sky.  I wouldn't be surprised if scientists finally discover that underneath all the quantum weirdness the universe is just playing a giant game of Go.

I spent some years doing nothing but playing Go, and in that time I discovered some fundamental things about life.  I am actually convinced that you can't learn something about Go without simultaneously learning something about life.  I have moved on to playing every other game ever made, but Go is always my first, before all things in its extreme elegance yet boundless depth.  I wish I had three lifetimes so I could spend two of them doing nothing but playing Go.  Thank you universe.

RATING: 10
Go is the purest abstract strategy game.  If this is your KIND of game, then it MUST be a 10.  Even if you haven't been able to master it to any level, its absolute perfection cannot be denied.  Chess and Shogi and other games possess certain arbitrary elements that Go simply does not have.  If you do not like this game its because either you don't like abstract strategy games and/or you value winning too much.  With Go there is always a bigger fish out there that will leave you crying for your Mommy.

The simplicity of it could be described by saying that life on other planets likely play Go too with exactly the same rules. Its just that intuitive.  Go was neither invented nor conceived by man.  It was discovered, just as the water molecule was discovered.

Mastering the game's subtleties would take a lifetime.

RATING: 10
[floatleft][ImageID=531635][/floatleft][b][u]Initial Impressions:[/u][/b]
"My God, it's full of stars..." 

[b][u]What I like:[/u][/b]
Go is the true game of games. Go is an entire universe in one game. Go is enjoyable at any skill level and the handicap system can bring players of differing skill levels into balance.

[b][u]What I dislike:[/u][/b]
This really isn't a dislike but I really wish Go was more well known in Western culture. I could care less if Go is popular but when someone looks at a chess board they know its chess. It would be nice if the same were true for Go.

[b][u]Overall:[/u][/b]
Go is my favorite game and more than likely it will always be my favorite game. 

Current rating ~10 kyu

The thing I find most exciting is how incredibly quickly the game can shift from offense to defense, and then back again.  The board is incredibly volatile in that sense, and it makes me interested just to see what's going to happen.

So far, I prefer playing on the smallest of the three standard board sizes.  It really isn't because it is "easier" on a smaller board.  Rather, we want to be able to complete a full game within the hour and find it doesn't happen on the larger board.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
As a pure logic brain-burner, this game is probably close to perfection. No hidden information, no randomness, virtually no setup, the same chances, broad replayability.

If you want to get better at the game, you're facing a lot of spatial pattern math studying. You need to understand consequences of certain not-yet-happenning moves and react before they happen. This asks for a meticulous board analysis that obviously leads to AP. In many ways "better" than chess, because as the game progresses the board state becomes more interesting, giving more options and choices - at least far until the end game / surrender. In chess, in mid game your options are somewhat limited and in the endgame, the state is less and less.

9x9 board is a perfect introduction and allow for relatively quick matches in a timespan that's in a casual board game range. 13x13 becomes more serious and can take significantly longer. Full game of 19x19 is something I want to stay away from - too long and too brain burning. Keep in mind that game on 19x19 is really a different game when compared to 13x13 or 9x9. 

For a new-age casual boardgamer, Go might be too tedious and too plain (visually and mechanically), the entry level for a beginner is easy, but to get any better is quite hard. I would probably recommend some easier/narrower games that follow a similar thinking pattern, like games from GIPF project or Gigamic games, like Pylos, Quarto, or even Blokus, perhaphs.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The one and only ... this is the greatest abstract ever made or possible.
This is an absolute 10, so that no other game in my Geeklist will be ever rated more than 9.

Oh, and it is so beautiful to see and touch !

"The board is a mirror of the mind of the players as the moments pass. When a master studies the record of a game he can tell at what point greed overtook the pupil, when he became tired, when he fell into stupidity, and when the maid came by with tea."
- Anonymous Go player

RATING: 9

The strategy is  extremely opaque - good German games have clearly defined short-term objectives so you have something to strive for, yet it reveals further levels as you play it more.

RATING: 10
This is the ultimate strategy game. Simple rules. Very susceptable to skill differences (more than 3 times more than chess) but with an effective handicap system. Player performance factors are very little to do with rote memorisation, yet are learnable. From the start each game is only about competing, rather than doing your own thing on the same board, with typical middle games that feel as if  a single wrong move would result in defeat. Fast games on a small board or longer games on a larger one. Why is this game so poorly known in the western world?

RATING: 10
The oldest game on Earth. The rules of the game make it so simple to play, but the strategies behind the moves are so incredibly deep. The more you play the game, the more you learn about what is behind every move. It's completely possible to get teachers to explain the game, in Eastern countries you can go to Go schools! But no matter how good any of us get - there will always be someone better. You can always learn something further about the game. The balance of the game is perfect too, with white getting 6.5 points to compensate for black getting the first move.

It's also a game that computers have not solved yet. While they can beat some of the lower level professionals - they cannot beat the top guys (yet!). 

I used to play tournament/league level chess, but after I discovered Go - I had no interest in going back to chess. Chess is just so limited a game compared to it!

Really the ultimate strategy game for me!

RATING: 8
Grueling learning curve, engrossing aftertaste. Each play is a treasure. I just wish I was better at it.

(+) Intuitive ruleset.
(+) Extraordinarily deep. Yes, the concept of game depth is actually correctly used here.
(+) Includes a handicap system to overcome differences in skill gaps of players.

(-) Almost requires immense investment in study to attain a level of proficiency. You can always play casually, of course, at the risk of the following:
(-) Dedicated players will, for lack of a better term, crush new players. Always. This isn't necessarily a negative point towards the design of the game, but more to reiterate what others have already mentioned to be true: this game is not particularly accessible for the masses.
(-) Not particularly exciting. Engrossing, certainly, just not exciting.

RATING: 10
The abstract strategy game which made me realize I can love abstract strategy games. A new cow-orker taught several of us in the mid 1990s (thanks Rob) and I was hooked immediately. Such surprising beautiful strategic depth arises from such simple minimalist rules. As one of the classics, it's easier to find opponents in real life and online than for most abstract strategy games, and the simple yet functional handicap system is a big advantage over most games. The long history and huge body of literature and culture significance is frosting on the cake.

While I lived in the US, I went to the US Go Congress each summer and most local tournaments. Now I play lots of other abstract strategy games in addition to Go, but I have no doubt I will be enjoying Go for the rest of my life.

RATING: 10
I used to rate go a "7" I really thought it was a great game, but I didn't dare put the time in to really knowing the game. I didn't want to become one dimensional in my gaming, and I had seen a lot of go players become just that. 

After several months like this I decided I would like to at least understand the game more. I purchased the first book in the Janice Kim series and started to study. I logged into KGS and started playing, found a teacher, and started to read more books.

I am to a point where Go takes up pretty much all of my online gaming time. I play on KGS and I read books and do problems. It hasn't replaced the multiplayer euros I enjoy, but it has replaced most of my 2 player gaming time.

RATING: 10
Though the rules are as simple as it gets, this game has one of the steepest learning curves in games. Your first game will be the least entertaining game of go you play. If you stick with it, the games get better. Every game peels back a new layer of strategy. Something will click and you feel you have a handle on what makes the game tick, only to have a new revelation a few games later. New strategies brings new enjoyment and new tension. This has happened again and again for years.  And in 40 years, I am quite certain I'll be saying the same thing. How many games can that be said about?

RATING: 10
Learned the game with a friend in California while I was in a minimum wage service job, and he was doing his PhD in Philosophy. He mocked me mercilessly about my skill as he trounced me repeatedly. I walked away so mad I couldn't see straight--ok, I could deal with someone making me look like an idiot, and teaching Heidegger to masters students while I was a mere cashier. But mocking my gaming skill!?! I Walked away straight into the local bookstore to buy the Ishida 7-volume Go book set, which I studied day and night for a week--pausing only to eat and sleep--in preparation for our next game. That's right, I called in sick to work for a week in order to study a game! I crushed him mercilessly when we played next, then began playing Go more seriously--that intense time of Go study had been completely addictive, I found I didn't want to let it go. We rarely played after that (I was too far beyond his level at that point), but without his mocking of my intellectual capacity and gameplaying ability I might never have taken up Go as much as I have to date, which I'm grateful for. Thanks, Sha!

since then play much more casually, but still have fun. Great game.

RATING: 10
Deep, fun, beautiful, and brain meltingly difficult. And I love the way conservative play is punished and inspiration rewarded.

Even as a newcomer, there's much more depth to the game than anything else that I have ever played, chess included. It's so refreshing to play a traditional game that not only holds its own against the best of the new stuff, but that also requires intelligent play and your full attention.

The existing body of commentary and strategy is fascinating, and focuses on pattern recognition and graceful, contemplative play. It's worth getting a book to start with, and I suggest 'Go for Beginners' by Iwamoto. As well as giving you all of the rules, and enough strategy to get you through the first 100 games or so, the book includes some interesting go problems, and some dan level games to replay at your leisure. A good companion book is Kano's 'Graded Go Problems for Beginners Vol 1' (Kiseido / Nihon Ki-in).

And don't bother with Chinese scoring... you'll have to learn Japanese style eventually, so you might as well jump right in. If you fear complexity this isn't your game, ne?

http://www.dragongoserver.net/ is an excellent turn-based (i.e. non-realtime PBEM style) go server that I highly recommend.

Currently double-digit kyu, and hopefully on the way up... the somewhat optimistic master plan is to hit 10k by the end of 2007. I'm happy to play / teach anyone interested in trying out the game. My pride and joy is my Japanese shin-kaya board and shell & slate stones.

Update: I did it. :)

New target: 5 kyu before I'm 40, shodan before I die.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is actually a very deep game, and I understand why some people rate it as highly as or higher than games such as chess. I've never played a more "positional" game than Go. The game is played on the intersections of a 19x19 grid board. Each player takes turns placing one piece of their color. You can play your piece on any empty intersection as long as it doesn't violate two rules which I'll mention in a second. Contiguously connected (non-diagonally) pieces are considered to be part of the same "army." If you place a piece so that it causes an opposing army to be entirely "surrounded" (that is, there is no play that will allow your opponent to increase that army in size), you capture those pieces and put them in front of you. The placement exceptions are: 1) Suicide. You can't place a piece if it would cause one of your armies (an army can be just one piece) to be captured immediately. 2) Infinity. You can't place a piece if it would cause repetition in the board layout (that is, if the board after your play would like like the board after your previous play). The game ends when no player has a move or (more likely) when both players concede the game is over. Dead zones are filled in, and pieces that both players acknowledge are doomed to be captured are captured right then and there. Then each player adds up their controlled (surrounded) spaces, and adds to that their captured pieces. The player with the higher point total wins. It's a very elegant game, but complex and sophisticated. Because of the many options, it's really not geared towards Western thinking. Excellent game, and if I was rating a game on any other scale I'd put this up to a 9 or 10, but on the BGG scale since I never bring this to the table the most I can give it is a 7.

RATING: 10
WHAT IS REALLY MINE

WHO'S AT THE TOP OF THE PILE

WHERE DOES HE DRAW THE LINE

LET THEM FIGURE IT OUT

GO ON AND STEP ACROSS

JUST REMIND YOURSELF

THAT WE ARE HERE TO GO

                        - Devo


Go is an abstract game, meaning what? It has simple rules, simple bits, and no overt theme. Place stones, capture stones with no remaining liberties, create living shapes to capture territory, and obey the ko rule. That's it. The thing is, go does have a rich theme, to me anyway. That theme can be a variety of things - it's really up to the player. Go is so satisying to me that I read books about it and solve go problems in my free time. I have dreams about it. When I was younger, I didn't get many opportunities to learn about it, unfortunately. But now, with things like the Kiseido Go Server, anyone with a computer can play pretty much anytime. Although not a big fan of playing games against other people on the computer, it's better than not playing at all.

So many things have been said about this game that it is hard to filter my own thoughts through all the commentary. If I could only play one game, this would be it. I like the way go tickles my mind - but even better, the game is incredibly exciting. I always found Chess to be kind of a turn off for me for some reason, and that prevented me from really investigating other abstract games. Years ago, I had played go a few times, and rated it about a 7 for a long time based on the memory of those games. Of course, I was a fool (that is, I robbed myself of playing more go, earlier!). I'm very glad that I dug a little deeper. Now I just have to be careful that I'm not consumed by my own passion for the game. :)

RATING: 3
Okay, here's the problem: this game is *too* deep.  You heard me.  The design on its own, in theory, is phenomenal.  Elegance in simplicity. "The perfect game" some call it. But then here's what happens in practice: you start playing it a lot, you develop a huge appreciation for its depth, you start reading about it, you want to play a lot... but, your friends, who apparently have way more free time than you, do exponentially more studying than you, they become very good at the game, and now, by comparison, you're even worse at the game than you were before.  And come on, how much fun can that be?  So, if you're willing to invest a ridiculous amount of time into it, it's a great game.  But it also means you may have deep unhappy issues and probably require some psychiatric counselling.

RATING: 10
Gets a 10 out of 10 for being the pinnacle of "mechanically simple but deeply strategic" elegance in game design.

More than any other game I have ever played it demands real study and attention. In many games you can present a reasonable facscimile of knowing what you're doing by playing tactically; in Go, not only will you lose badly, in your early games you're unlikely to even see what you could have done better.

I love it, but I don't want to expend the time and effort required to improve.

{Abstract; area-control} Go is my most favorite abstract game. It's very deep and absolutely unforgiving. To play it well, you need to read books, learn from good teachers, watch many games from advanced players, remember classic patterns and train for 4 years. 

Many theories about Go have been established. The strategies keep evolving.

I hate people saying that Go is invented in Japan.  It's invented in China!

RATING: 10
[u]Key Words:[/u] Abstract Strategy - Mind-bogglingly Amazing - 2-Player - 1h

[u]Summarized Opinion:[/u] My favorite game. 11/10.

[u]My Rating Explained:[/u] Outstanding. This game has given me countless hours of fun. I fully expect to keep playing this for the rest of my life.

[u]Verbose Assessment:[/u]

I am a fortunate gamer in the sense that I already found my 'ultimate' game, and that game is Go.

I could talk about the thrill a close or hard-fought game can cause.

I could talk about the nigh infinite variations that come up to keep the game interesting and fresh.

I could talk about the pride you feel after a particularly sharp move or a just won game against an even-matched opponent.

I could talk about the sense of accomplishment you feel as you make progress.

But all that really doesn't do the game justice. You owe it to yourself to find someone who can show you how it works, in real life or online, and discover Go.

RATING: 8.1
This game is a 3M Bookshelf Classic game - it was manufactured in 1973. GO is probably the world's oldest board game in existence. It originated in China and various references date it back as far as 2356 B.C. It was brought to Japan about 1000-1200 years ago where it soon became the game for feudal lords, nobles and eventually the common people. Today it is Japan's most popular board game. We have the complete game consisting of the intersections and the stones, with explicit directions for strategies and rules of the game. It is all housed in this sturdy box so that it fits on a bookshelf. The picture on the front is from the Art Institute of Chicago "The Clarence Buckingham Collection of Japanese Prints, which show several Japanese ladies playing the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Go is a very simple game. You can easily teach it to a 6 year old child. But it is also the deepest and most complex game ever. You can devote your entire life to the game and still not master it.

The thing that is so magical about Go is that both the 6 year old newbie and the 60 year old professional grand master will have just as fun with the game. The ingenious handicap system allows you to play opponents of various skill and still have an even and exciting game.

Some people claim that Go requires to much study and time to play. That is nonsense. You can play it just as casually as any other game. The "problem" is that you WANT to get better at Go. The game has a bizarre power. You want to master it, even though you know that you never will.

Try it! Start small. A 9x9 board is enough in the beginning. It plays really fast and teaches you tactics and "close combat". Soon you'll be ready to tackle the "normal" board, 19x19. That's when you discover the real magic of Go. Now the game evolves into a strategy game.

Play it to have fun and to improve yourself - whether you win or lose.

1) Chess-board-sized cardboard board, small plastic stones.
2) Plywood board, plastic stones.

RATING: 5
"New Style Box" edition of the game from the 1960s. The box has noticeable amount of wear with the edges having some whitening and some parts of the art having been chipped over the years (the most noticeably from where a price sticker was removed by previous owner). One corner of the top half of the box is split but the bottom remains in great condition.

Inside the box is a folded 19x19 wooden board (with point markers for a 13x13 set up too) with hinge and clasp to keep closed. The folded board is hollow which allows it to also act as storage for the stones with each color having their own cardboard box with art. The boxes for the stones each have a split corner on the top half, but the bottom half remains in great condition similar to the outer box.

The actual game contents are near perfect. The board has 2 small parts where the paint for the lines has faded and 1 or 2 stones of each color has a slight blemish, but none of it effects the ability to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Many plays decades ago. The greatest game ever created. The state of mind it creates between the players is of profound nuance and transport.

When I was a young man, I was introduced to Go by an art professor. She had a studio filled with Red Grooms paintings from her youth, a plaza open to the Florida night sky. We played Go almost daily for over a year. Our play formed me and remains a pristine memory, a ritual marker at my transition to adulthood, something so solid and uncorrupted I can slip into that state of mind.

I no longer play. It would not be wrong to set aside all modern board games and pick up Go. Maybe one day, like Peg, I can show someone young how to live well, how the aesthetics of the mind can be mirrored over something as simple as choosing a place for a stone, the responsive snap.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Several sets, including shell&slate stones in wood bowls w/ 2-1/2 inch board from trip to Finger Lakes NY (Canandaigua) - Also own Yunzi stones size 32 and a 2-1/2 inch bamboo board, plus a nice magnetic travel set, 3M Go, and others.  Just got a set of 42 size Yunzi stones (cleaned/oiled/polished) and Kaya bowls from YMI that are BEAUTIFUL.  I was disappointed to discover my Canandaigua board is too small for 23mm stones. But, my bamboo board, and 9x9/13x13 practice board, fit standard stones perfectly, so I'm okay! 

Go is probably the single best 2 player abstract strategy game there is, though the very best players will convince you the game is less abstract than it seems, and actually teaches military level strategy. The ideas of surrounding and capturing territory by complete enclosure, prevented by the formation of 2 eyes, comprises the totality of the game's objectives.  Regional shape is often used to describe game strategy. The complexity is exposed with the huge disparity between, and multiple levels of, game-play ability. Some players devote their lives to this game. I just dabble at it. (I also have collected a decent sized Go Library.)

RATING: 10
Endless depth and one of the greatest games ever created. The simplicity of the rules makes this a marvel and a complete game. The scoring at the end is problematic, but at worse, you play it out till it's really obvious.

It's quite difficult to get to the table since you need a dedicated partner, and teaching people from the ground up isn't simple either. There's so much to teach and a lot is dependent on your own personal style and your ability to grasp the "feel" of the game. I'm not good enough to be able to pinpoint exactly why something is a good move or not except through gut instinct. Mind you, the number of terrible moves I make far outweigh the number of good moves.

Tactics and strategy abound in this abstract, and it disappoints me when people say that they refuse to play this because it's not thematic enough; use your imagination!

RATING: 10
Go or Chess? Go. I like Go better because it's simpler. Because it's easier for a novice to begin playing fruitfully. Because no piece is better than any other. Go is quite possibly the best boardgame ever devised (on this planet, anyway); however, there are just as many folk who would put Chess on that pedestal. Both games are relatively simple to learn, but take a lifetime to master; but in my own personal experience, I would say that Go is more fun for the novice player for exactly that reason. With Chess, as a novice you reach the point very quickly where, in order to "seriously" play the game you pretty much have to devote a serious amount of time to the game. In my personal experience, Go's suitability as a recreational game, where the novice can fruitfully have fun playing the game, even against much better players, is higher than Chess.

RATING: 10
Ok, I am in deep enough to finally feel I can rate this.  I have worked past the initial ramp up, and can say it is worth it.  I have played online at KGS enough to be rated at 18 kyu (I'd guess that means no longer a 'noob' but still a beginner).  GO is complex enough to remain unsolvable by today's computers, but simple enough to learn in a few minutes.  It can be frustrating in that you can't believe there is much to learn, but you are soundly beaten by stronger players.  A "mental martial art" is a good analogy since it takes a long time to gain mastery at GO.  If you put a little effort into this game, it can repay you with a lifetime of entertainment.  Solid 10 for me!

RATING: 6
Undoubtedly a brilliant game, worth of study, etc., but that's the problem (for me): it's too deep. It can't really be played casually (enough to enjoy it, which implies some comprehension of what you're doing). It also distinctly lacks angst, or sharp decision points, making it an incremental contest without much of the drama of its great rival, Chess. I think much of the allure here comes from the ability to play poorly but still accomplish *something*, a surprisingly modern trait for such an ancient game.

RATING: 8
Go is a beautiful and fascinating game that I mess around with studying periodically.  Someday when I go through my drastic game-life simplification, it'll be one of the few I keep around, just for its endless depth.

That said, I don't find it as satisfying to play in person as the other abstract behemoths, in particular the chess family of games.  For some reason, moving pieces on a board just tickles my brain and sensibilities more than placing stones.  Thus, I find I like to study go more than play it, and when I get a hankering for a longer abstract, it's more likely to be chess or xiangqi than go.

RATING: 10
My favorite game. I have played thousands of games of it, and am looking forward to thousands more. It requires an amazing mix of tactical puzzle solving, long term strategy, and intuition to play well. It's biggest flaw is that it has a brutal learning curve; the rules can be explained in a few minutes, but it usually takes several plays until you can grasp things enough to complete a game without help, then it takes months or years of regular play before you get past the beginner stage. This is a game for a lifelong hobby, not something you'll play a few times, enjoy, then put away.

Liked it so much I wrote an online server for go, http://www.gokgs.com/ (the KGS Go Server).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
If there had to be one game only, I'd vote for Go, even though I prefer to play Chess. It is not as tough on beginners as chess, and beginners should get gratification from their second game at latest (5 stones handicap on a 9x9).

That being said, I like the concept, but I do not like the rules. The Chinese ones are well defined, but they have hidden problems, and counting the Japanese way is much quicker. Reading the Japanese rules, however, makes you appreciate the clarity and elegance of Chess rules. If you doubt that, then do what almost no Go player did and read them.

The weak point of Go is that it really shines on large boards and on large boards only. On smaller boards, it is just ok. Compared to other boardgames, the Go-bang is ridiculously oversized and gives overlong games. I subtract a point for that.

RATING: 9.5
I'd like to give this a 10, but really, I can see myself turning this down if I don't feel like it. Also, the rift between the newer player and the more experienced player is bigger than the Grand Canyon. That might be seen as a good thing, but basically it means that whenever I do feel like playing this, I won't have an opponent of the same or better strength. Other people I play against don't like being handicapped. I beat a friend with a handicap of 30 stones once because he gave up after I captured one of them.

Edit: Upgraded because I've been wanting to play this more again lately
